DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals reversing the decision of the Workers Compensation Board is affirmed. Decision of the Workers Compensation Board is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Kansas), which reversed a decision of the Workers Compensation Board, which concluded that the worker had not established causation proving that his work-related injuries had worsened.


OVERVIEW: The employee was injured during the course and scope of employment and was awarded temporary total disability under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 44-528. The employee sought review and contended that his injuries had worsened. The administrative law judge awarded the employee additional compensation, but the Board reversed the award and held that the employee had not established causation proving that his injuries had suffered more than normal aging. The appellate court reversed the Board's decision and affirmed the decision of the administrative law judge. Kan. Stat. Ann. § 44-528 permitted modification of awards in order to conform to changed conditions to the employee's injuries. The court found that, when an initial medical condition itself progressed into complications more serious than the original injury, the added complications were compensable. The court held that the appellate court was permitted to disturb the Board's decision because it determined that the Board failed to follow the prescribed procedures. The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the appellate court.

 

40 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. McAffry


Nos. 78,820, 78,821


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


263 Kan. 521; 949 P.2d 1137; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 179



December 12, 1997, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Linn district court; RICHARD M. SMITH, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a judgment from the Linn District Court (Kansas) that determined that Kan. Stat. Ann. § 32-1003(g) was unconstitutionally overbroad.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with violating Kan. Stat. Ann. § 32-1003(g), which prohibited the use of an artificial light to locate an animal while having a rifle, pistol, shotgun, bow, or other implement in their possession. The trial court found that § 32-1003(g) was unconstitutional because the statute used the term "animal." Thus, the trial court found that the statute made it a crime to shine a spotlight on a dog from a vehicle in which a weapon was located. The State appealed and argued that the legislature intended the term "animal" to mean "wildlife" animals. The court disagreed and affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court found that legislature used the term "wildlife" 28 times throughout Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 32-1002 - 32-1005. Thus, an average person could conclude that the term "animal" meant something other than "wildlife" as it appeared in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 32-1003(g). Further, whether the legislature intended to use the term "wildlife" was not as important as how the average person would understand the language of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

State v. Barnes


No. 77,073


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


263 Kan. 249; 948 P.2d 627; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 160



November 7, 1997, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised November 25, 1997.
Decision reached on appeal by State v. Barnes, 366 P.3d 650, 2016 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 48 (Kan. Ct. App., 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Sedgwick district court, CLARK V. OWENS, II, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions for first-degree murder and aggravated kidnapping and the hard 40 sentence imposed from the District Court of Sedgwick County, claiming that the Kansas competence statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3302, was unconstitutional and that the evidence failed to establish his competence. He also contended the trial court erred in its instructions and its consideration of evidence upon sentencing.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant for the kidnapping and murder of a teenager who was going to testify against him in another case. On appeal, defendant argued that because the procedure for determining whether a criminal defendant was competent under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3302 did not allow him the opportunity to cross-examine court-appointed physicians, he was denied due process of law. The court reviewed the procedure and concluded that § 22-3302 provided defendant with an adequate procedure; and, therefore, was constitutional. The court then reviewed the factors that the trial court considered in determining that defendant was competent to stand trial and held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in making that determination. Defendant also argued that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury on self-defense, but after reviewing the record, the court held that there was simply no evidence that would allow a jury to determine that the defendant acted in self-defense. The record also established that the trial court did consider mitigation evidence required by § 21-4635(b) and concluded that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in sentencing defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of first-degree murder and aggravated kidnapping and the hard 40 sentence imposed.

State v. Cellier


No. 74,976


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


263 Kan. 54; 948 P.2d 616; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 144



October 31, 1997, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised November 25, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Lyon district court; JOHN O. SANDERSON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Lyon District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of first-degree, premeditated murder and aggravated kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had conceived a plan to take a victim to a remote area to interrogate him after shooting him with a shotgun. He had been told by a friend that such actions would result in death. Defendant challenged the admissibility of four statements, the voluntariness of his Miranda rights waiver, his competency to stand trial, the constitutionality of the competency statute, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3201, 22-3302, and the sufficiency of the evidence to find him sane when he shot the victim. The court determined that defendant had failed to preserve for appeal his challenges to the statements and waiver because he had not properly objected at trial. At defendant's competency hearing, two doctors testified that he understood the proceedings or could assist in his defense. The court found the competency statute constitutional because the district court could measure the evidence by the preponderance of evidence standard. The district court had not err in determining that defendant had not proven that he was incompetent to stand trial based on the evidence and its observation of him at the hearing. The jury could find defendant sane at the time of the shooting and guilty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the district court.

Post-conviction relief denied at Lee v. State, 222 P.3d 564, 2010 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 18 (Kan. Ct. App., Jan. 15, 2010)
Related proceeding at Lee v. Kansas, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 150525 (D. Kan., Oct. 19, 2012)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Lee v. State, 386 P.3d 527, 2016 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1006 (Kan. Ct. App., Dec. 16, 2016)

Injured Workers v. Franklin


No. 77,142, No. 77,561


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


262 Kan. 840; 942 P.2d 591; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 134



July 18, 1997, Opinion filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised August 5, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 77,142, appeal from Shawnee district court; CHARLES E. ANDREWS, JR., judge. No. 77,561, appeal from Workers Compensation Board.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant workers appealed from a judgment of the District Court, Shawnee County (Kansas), which held in favor of appellee secretary of human resources and appellee director of workers compensation in an action alleging that the state Workers Compensation Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 44-510d, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The workers brought an action against the secretary of human resources and the director of workers compensation, alleging that the state Workers Compensation Act (Act), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 44-510d was unconstitutional. The trial court held in favor of the secretary of human resources and the director of workers compensation, holding that the Act was constitutional, and the workers appealed. The court ruled that determining whether a statute violated the constitution was a question of law and that when determining a question of law, the court could exercise an unlimited de novo standard of review. Further, the court held that a statute was presumed constitutional, and all doubts must have been resolved in favor of its validity. The court affirmed the judgment holding the Act constitutional, finding that the Act provided compensation for injured workers without proof of negligence or fault, a benefit not allowed under typical tort law. The court concluded that while several amendments of the Act did not restrict an employee's right to receive workers compensation benefits, several other amendments were enacted with the intent to expand an employee's rights.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of the secretary of human resources and the director of workers compensation was affirmed.

State v. Heironimus


No. 77,733


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


262 Kan. 796; 941 P.2d 1356; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 113



July 11, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Reno district court; STEVEN R. BECKER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a decision by the Reno District Court (Kansas), which declared the habitual violator statutes (HVS), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-284 et seq., unconstitutional for lack of due process and dismissed a complaint against defendant without prejudice.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was declared a habitual violator under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-286 (1996 Supp.), and was charged with operating a motor vehicle while his driving privileges were revoked under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 8-287 (1996 Supp.), a severity Level 9, non-person felony. At the preliminary hearing, defendant challenged the constitutionality of the HVS, claiming a due process violation because the HVS did not provide for a pre-revocation administrative hearing. The issue was whether the HVS were unconstitutional because they failed to provide due process. The court found that the HVS constitutional and reversed the district court. Defendant received the process he was due. The court ruled that the Due Process Clause had never been construed to require that the procedures used to guard against an erroneous deprivation of a protectible "property" or "liberty" interest be so comprehensive as to preclude any possibility of error. The Due Process Clause simply did not mandate that all governmental decision-making comply with standards that assured perfect, error-free determinations.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

Kansas Pub. Emples. Retirement Sys. v. Reimer & Koger Assocs.


No. 76,524


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


262 Kan. 635; 941 P.2d 1321; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 109



June 27, 1997, Opinion filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised June 27, 1997. As Revised August 5, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Shawnee district court, FRANKLIN R. THEIS, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant investment fiduciaries challenged the order of the Shawnee District Court (Kansas), which denied their motions for summary judgment, finding that appellee retirement system's investment activity was a governmental and not a proprietary function, and therefore, no statute of limitations applied. Retirement system made claim for monies lost in investments.


OVERVIEW: Retirement system suffered significant losses due to the actions of investment fiduciaries. It made claim for return of that money, and the investment fiduciaries asserted that the claims were barred by the statute of limitations. The court held that the trial court's ruling refusing to grant summary judgment to the defendants was proper. Retirement system was asserting a public right or protecting property held for public use; therefore, its actions were all governmental in character, to which no statute of limitations applied prior to the enactment of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-522, which provided a 10-year statute of limitations. Since the legislature intended § 60-522 to be applied retroactively, any time barred claims would have been revived. Collateral estoppel did not prevent litigation of the claim because the purposes of collateral estoppel did not exist, and its application would have fostered injustice and allowed allowing a federal court to preclude consideration of its erroneous determination of state law. Section 60-522(c) did not violate the Kansas Constitution.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court that denied investment fiduciaries' motions for summary judgment was affirmed.

State ex rel. State Bd. of Healing Arts v. Beyrle


No. 77,411


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


262 Kan. 507; 941 P.2d 371; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 100



May 30, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; DAVID W. DEWEY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant naturopath sought an interlocutory appeal of an order of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which entered partial judgment declaring Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-2872a unconstitutional, but specifically reserved judgment on whether the naturopath unlawfully prescribed drugs or exceeded his statutory authority.


OVERVIEW: Appellee State, on behalf of the State Board of Healing Arts, sued the naturopath, contending that the naturopath violated the Healing Arts Act. The Board requested to have the naturopath prohibited by injunction or quo warranto from engaging in acts in violation of the Healing Arts Act and to have Kan. Stat. Ann. 65-2872a declared unconstitutional. The trial court granted the Board's motion for partial judgment and declared the statute unconstitutional. The court dismissed the naturopath's appeal, holding that: (1) the journal entry of judgment was not an appealable final judgment because the order only involved a determination the constitutionality of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 65-2872a and there was no adjudication of whether the naturopath had actually violated any of the provisions of the Healing Arts Act; (2) the court lacked jurisdiction to hear the case under an exception to the final judgment rule because no attempt was made to satisfy the requirements of Kan. Ann. § 60-2102(a)(2) because the trial court did not grant either quo warranto relief or an injunction.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the naturopath's attempted appeal of the trial court's order declaring the naturopathy statute unconstitutional.

 

48 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. Taylor


No. 76,557


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


262 Kan. 471; 939 P.2d 904; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 102



May 30, 1997, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; DAVID W. KENNEDY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal sustained and case remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of aggravated escape. The Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) excluded certain prior convictions from defendant's criminal history, which resulted in a criminal history classification of "I" and a sentence of eight months' imprisonment. The State of Kansas appealed upon a question reserved, pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-3602(b)(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to nine criminal charges that were brought in three separate complaints, which had been consolidated for trial. He was placed on probation in a community corrections facility. Defendant later pleaded guilty to escaping from the facility. The State conceded that defendant's cocaine possession conviction could not be counted in determining his criminal history. However, the State asserted that the remaining eight convictions should be counted. The district court found that the convictions could not be counted due to the fact that they had been consolidated with a conviction that was used as an element of the charged offense. On appeal, the court noted that a prior conviction that was used as an element of a subsequently charged offense could not be used in determining the criminal history category for that offense, but that all other prior convictions were to be used, unless prohibited by statute. The court thus found that the trial court erred in failing to use the eight convictions because none were used as an element of the escape charge, there was no statutory prohibition to their use, and they were otherwise applicable in scoring the criminal history.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the State's appeal on the question reserved and remanded the matter for resentencing.

 

49 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ninci


No. 74,725


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


262 Kan. 21; 936 P.2d 1364; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 57



April 18, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court; WILLIAM A. CLEAVER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Johnson District Court (Kansas), which entered judgment of his convictions for felony murder, in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401 (1992 Supp.), robbery, in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3426 (1988), and aggravated burglary, in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3716 (1992 Supp.).


OVERVIEW: After a jury trial, defendant was convicted of first-degree felony murder, robbery, and aggravated burglary. Defendant was sentenced to life for the murder conviction, and to 5 to 20 years each for the robbery and aggravated burglary convictions, with the sentences to run consecutively. Upon review, the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that all evidence that derived from defendant's confession was admissible because defendant was not in custody for the hour that led up to his confession; thus, as there was no custodial interrogation, there was no requirement for the police to read him his Miranda rights. Further, the court held that the police was not required to clarify defendant's ambiguous request for counsel before he signed a consent to search form, and they were allowed to ignore the request without violating defendant's Fifth Amendment right to counsel; thus, the evidence that was obtained as result the consent form was admissible. The court also found no abuse of discretion in the district court's decision to admit three autopsy photos into evidence because none of the photos were repetitious or without probative value.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Weinlood v. Simmons


No. 77,916


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


262 Kan. 259; 936 P.2d 238; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 70



April 18, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court, FRANKLIN R. THEIS, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, plaintiff inmates filed suit against defendant Secretary of the Kansas Department of Corrections and challenged the assessment of service fees against them pursuant to Kan. Admin. Regs. 44-5-115. The Shawnee District Court (Kansas), entered a judgment in the inmates' favor. The Secretary appealed.


OVERVIEW: In an action filed pursuant to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, the inmates challenged the assessment of service fees against them pursuant to Kan. Admin. Regs. 44-5-115. The trial court held that the service fees for administering the inmates' trust accounts violated their due process rights and that Kan. Admin. Regs. 44-5-115(a) exceeded the statutory authority granted in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 75-52-139, because it ordered the fees to be paid to the crime victims compensation fund. The trial court ordered the refunding of the service fees paid after the action was filed and granted a permanent injunction against the future collection of those service fees. The Secretary appealed. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the case with directions to enter judgment in the Secretary's favor. The court held that the Secretary had a right to require the inmates to reimburse the state for their keep and maintenance, and that the assessment of a monthly service fee for administering the trust accounts did not violate their due process rights. Also, the court held that the inmates were not entitled to injunctive relief because they had no private interests distinct from that of the public at large.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case with directions.

Johnson v. Kansas Pub. Emples. Retirement Sys.


No. 76,374


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


262 Kan. 185; 935 P.2d 1049; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 79



April 18, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court, ADRIAN J. ALLEN and JAMES P. BUCHELE, judges.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee employees filed an action against appellants, Kansas Public Employees Retirement System (KPERS) and City of Manhattan, Kansas, challenging a change to their pension benefits under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The Shawnee District Court (Kansas) converted the action to a continuing breach of contract action and awarded damages to the employees. The district court found that the City was liable to indemnify the KPERS. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Due to a consolidation, the employees, police officers, were transferred from the employ of the City to the Riley County, Kansas, Law Enforcement Agency. Their pension benefits changed from being administered by the City to being administered by the KPERS. The employees were required to make higher employee contributions under the KPERS system. Sixteen years after the change, the employees brought their 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action in federal court. The federal court dismissed the action and venue was transferred to the district court. The district court found that there was a five-year statute of limitations for the employees' action under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-511, but that because appellants had engaged in a continuing contract violation, the employees were entitled to damages for the previous five years. On appeal, the court held that the employees' action was barred by the statute of limitations. The court ruled that the employees' action accrued when the alleged breach occurred, which the court set at the date of the employees' first paycheck under the KPERS system. The court determined that there was no continuing contract violation as there was no continuing contract.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the action with instructions to dismiss the employee's claim.

State v. Sims


No. 76,205


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS

Post-conviction relief denied at Sims v. State, 131 P.3d 1280, 2006 Kan. App. LEXIS 338 (Kan. Ct. App., 2006)
Post-conviction relief denied at Sims v. State, 2015 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 460 (Kan. Ct. App., June 5, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court, DAVID W. KENNEDY, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which convicted him of one count of first-degree murder, one count of criminal discharge of a firearm at an occupied building, one count of criminal possession of a firearm, and two counts of aggravated battery, resulting from his participation in a drive-by shooting.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted him of first-degree murder, criminal discharge of a firearm at an occupied building, criminal possession of a firearm, and aggravated battery, resulting from his participation in a drive-by shooting. The trial court refused to give a self-defense instruction requested by defendant. Defendant contended that admission of gang affiliation was error. The court found that defendant did not object to the introduction of the gang evidence at trial and, therefore, the issue was not properly preserved for appeal. Some of the complained-of testimony was elicited in response to questions during cross-examination. The trial court properly denied defendant' request for a self-defense instruction because there was absolutely no evidence proving that defendant honestly believed that he had to kill in self-defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions.

State v. Robinson


No. 75,808


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


261 Kan. 865; 934 P.2d 38; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 40



March 7, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Franklin district court; THOMAS H. SACHSE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, challenged his conviction for depraved heart second-degree murder in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3402(b) entered by the Franklin District Court (Kansas).


OVERVIEW: On the day he died, the victim told police that he intended to take care of some boys who were threatening his sons. Thereafter, he found defendant and three other boys in a park and started a fight. The victim chased the boys with a baseball bat. Subsequently, each boy grabbed a golf club and started chasing the victim. When one boy fell, the victim hit him twice with the bat. Defendant subsequently hit the victim in the head with his club, the club stuck in the victim's head, and defendant ran away. At the police station, defendant gave a second confession to another officer after waiving his Miranda rights again. The court held that the evidence was sufficient for the jury to find that defendant recklessly killed the victim while manifesting an extreme indifference to the value of the victim's life and that § 21-3402(b) was not unconstitutionally vague. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

54 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


Lemuz by & Through Lemuz v. Fieser


No. 76,624


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


261 Kan. 936; 933 P.2d 134; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 45



March 7, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification of a question of law from the United States District Court for the District of Kansas, FRANK G. THEIS, judge.



DISPOSITION: The question certified is determined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of Kansas certified the question of whether Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 40-3403(h) and 65-442(b) violated Kan. Const. B. of R. §§ 1 or 18, as a result of plaintiff minor patient's and parents' opposition to defendant medical center's motion for summary judgment in plaintiffs' medical malpractice action against defendant physician, treating physician, and medical center.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, minor patient and parents, brought a medical malpractice action against defendant, physician, treating physician, and medical center. Defendant medical center moved for summary judgment pursuant to Kan. Rev. Stat. §§ 40-3403(h) and 65-442(b), and the lower court certified the question as to whether those sections violated Kan. Const. B. of R. §§ 1 or 18. The court held that § 40-3403(h) did not violate §1 because it bore a reasonable relationship to the legislature's objectives, and did not violate § 18, because the Health Care Provider Insurance Availability Act (Act), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 40-3401 et seq., was an adequate quid pro quo for the abrogation of the common-law remedy of vicarious liability against hospitals. The court also held that § 65-442(b) did not violate § 18, because it too, bore a reasonable relationship to the legislature's objectives, and that the Act, the Health Care Provider Insurance Availability Plan, and the Health Care Stabilization Fund, supplemented by the risk management statutes, Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 65-4922 and 65-4923, created an adequate quid pro quo for the abrogation of plaintiffs' corporate negligence remedy.


OUTCOME: The court held that Kan. Stat. Ann. §§ 40-3403(h) and 65-442(b), did not violate Kan. Const. B. of R. § 18, and that § 40-3403(h), did not violate Kan. Const. B. of R. § 1, because both statutes bore a reasonable relationship to the legislature's objectives, and because the quid pro quo remedies for the abrogation of plaintiffs' common-law remedies were adequate.

 

55 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


In re Kaul


No. 74,528


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


261 Kan. 755; 933 P.2d 717; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 39



March 7, 1997, Opinion filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended March 12, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Jackson district court; GARY L. NAFZIGER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants county appraiser and commissioners challenged the judgment by the Jackson District Court (Kansas), which affirmed the Board of Tax Appeals' determination that appellee landowner's property within the formal boundaries of an Indian reservation was exempt from ad valorem taxes.


OVERVIEW: The landowner was an enrolled member of an Indian tribe. The landowner's property was located within the reservation. The county assessed ad valorem taxes against the property. The landowner protested. The Board ordered the assessment removed, and held that the land was exempt pursuant to the 1861 Act for the Admission of Kansas into the Union. The county appealed. The trial court affirmed. The county again appealed. The court reversed and remanded. The court held that if the State's laws permitted it, there was no impediment to ad valorem taxation of privately owned land within the bounds of a reservation. All Indian land was excluded from the boundaries of the State pursuant to the 1861 Act. The Treaty of 1861, 12 Stat. 1191, included a provision that if tribal land was assigned in fee simple, the land became subject to ad valorem taxes. Provisions in the Treaty of 1867, 15 Stat. 531, which stated that tribal lands were not within the boundaries of the state, did not apply to the landowner's property. Because the source of the landowner's fee patent was unclear and because there could have been other treaties which impacted the land, the case was remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which had affirmed the Board's determination that the landowner's property was exempt from ad valorem taxation by the county. The court remanded for further proceedings.

Williams Natural Gas Co. v. Supra Energy


No. 74,690


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


261 Kan. 624; 931 P.2d 7; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 21



January 31, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Elk district court; JOHN E. SANDERS, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of the decision of the Elk District Court (Kansas), which granted a temporary injunction to appellee gas company permitting it to perform testing on the corporation's gas wells.


OVERVIEW: A temporary injunction, which was granted pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 55-1210, permitted a gas company to conduct testing on a corporation's gas wells that were located adjacent to the company's storage fields. On appeal, the corporation challenged the constitutionality of the testing provisions found in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 55-1210(c)(2) and (3) and the right to injunctive relief under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 55-1210(d). The corporation's storage field was an integral part of its pipeline system, in which substantial amounts of gas were injected, stored, and withdrawn. The company leased property adjacent to the storage filed on which it had several oil and gas wells. The court found that there was nothing unconstitutional about permitting anyone to be considered an injector for purposes of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 55-1210. The statute did not entitled injectors to condemn property and the testing provisions did not constitute a taking of property. Thus the testing provisions were constitutional. The court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in granting the temporary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

57 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


In re Boeing Co.


No. 75,702


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


261 Kan. 508; 930 P.2d 1366; 1997 Kan. LEXIS 18



January 24, 1997, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; D. KEITH ANDERSON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the district court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a municipal improvement district and a board of county commissioners, challenged a judgment of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which held that the Improvement District Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 19-2753 et seq., specifically Kan. Stat. Ann. § 19-2765(a)(9), authorized levying general taxes assessed on an ad valorem basis against appellee taxpayer's personal property to pay bonds issued for a sewer lateral reconstruction project.


OVERVIEW: With the approval of the district, the taxpayer built its own sewer trunk line. A few years later, voters approved bond issues financed by ad valorem taxes on all taxable property in the district for the reconstruction of sewer laterals in a residential area within the district. Although the taxpayer's real property was exempt, the cost of the project, through taxes assessed on its personal property, fell primarily on the taxpayer. The taxpayer argued that the district's sewer lateral reconstruction project provided no "benefit" to the taxpayer's properties under § 19-2765(a)(9). However, the court held that, although there was no direct benefit to the taxpayer, the taxpayer's personal property received indirect benefits from the project even though they were slight compared to the taxes assessed. The court noted that the project was to prevent sewage overflows as had occurred in the past on a street that served the taxpayer's property. The court further explained that § 19-2765(a)(9) did not specify how much benefit had to be received by the taxpayer and that the entire district benefitted from reduced maintenance costs for the streets and sewers resulting from the project.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.

Bonin v. Vannaman


No. 75,014


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


261 Kan. 199; 929 P.2d 754; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 168



December 20, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court; JAMES FRANKLIN DAVIS, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant daughter sought review of a decision of the District Court of Johnson County (Kansas), which granted the motions to dismiss of appellees, doctor and parents. The district court granted appellee doctor's motion to dismiss pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-515. Appellant's complaint alleged acts of malpractice and fraud by appellee doctor and acts of negligence by appellee parents.


OVERVIEW: Appellant daughter suffered from a sever disability associated with scoliosis. Appellant brought an action versus appellees, doctor and parents, in the district court. Appellant's complaint alleged acts of malpractice and fraud by appellee doctor and acts of negligence by appellee parents. The district court granted the motions to dismiss of appellees, doctor and parents. The district court granted appellee doctor's motion to dismiss pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-515. The court affirmed the district court's judgment. The court held that appellant's fraud claim versus appellee doctor was invalid, as it was a negligence claim in substance and thus barred by Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-515(a); the eight-year statute of repose in § 60-515(a) was constitutional, as it did not violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV or §§ 1, 18 of the Kan. Const. Bill of Rights; the continuous treatment doctrine was not recognized in the context of a medical malpractice action; and that appellant's action versus appellee parents was barred because appellee parents was entitled to parental immunity related to their decision not to investigate appellant's medical condition prior to the running of § 60-515.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's grant of the motions to dismiss of appellees, doctor and parents, related to the action brought against appellees by appellant daughter. The court held that appellant's fraud claim versus appellee doctor was barred by the applicable state statute, as it was in substance a negligence claim; and that appellant's claim versus appellee parents was barred, as appellee parents was entitled to parental immunity.

Peden v. State Dep't of Revenue


No. 75,205


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


261 Kan. 239; 930 P.2d 1; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 170



December 20, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; MATTHEW J. DOWD, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged an order from the Shawnee County District Court (Kansas), which held that a tax rate schedule under the Kansas Income Tax Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3201 et seq., was unconstitutional to the extent it imposed tax rates on single taxpayers that were higher than the highest tax rate imposed on married taxpayers filing jointly. The trial court also held that it had subject matter jurisdiction to hear plaintiff taxpayer's claim.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer filed an action challenging a tax rate schedule under the Kansas Income Tax Act (KITA), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3201 et seq., to the extent that it imposed tax rates on single taxpayers that were higher than the highest tax rate imposed on married taxpayers filing jointly. The trial court found that the tax rate schedule was unconstitutional and that it had subject matter jurisdiction over plaintiff's tax refund claim. Upon review, the court reversed. The court determined the tax schedule was not unconstitutional because the discriminatory classification was rationally related to the state's legitimate interest of promoting marriage. The court noted that under the rational basis standard, a close fit or mathematical perfection between a legislative goal and the means chosen to further that goal was not required. The court found that it was rational for the state to speculate that favorable tax treatment for married taxpayers in the form of lower rates would encourage single taxpayers to get married, thereby fulfilling a valid state objective. The court concluded that it was not necessary for the state to put forth hard data to prove its speculation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order that held a tax schedule that imposed tax rates on single taxpayers that were higher than the highest tax rate imposed on married taxpayers filing jointly was unconstitutional.

OVERVIEW: Claimant was purportedly injured while working at a nursing home after being struck by a patient. Regarding claimant's constitutional challenges to an amendment to the workers compensation law, the court held that the revised appeals procedure enacted by the state legislature in response to a court decision did not deny claimant's due process rights. The court further held, in affirming the board's decision, that claimant's remaining arguments, including an allegation that the amended statutes violated the separation of powers doctrine, were without merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the board, and also found no merit to claimant's contentions challenging the constitutionality of the workers compensation law.

 

61 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. Neufeld


No. 74,412


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 930; 926 P.2d 1325; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 152



November 8, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Shawnee district court; JAMES P. BUCHELE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from an order of the Shawnee District Court (Kansas), which granted defendant, a state representative's, motion to dismiss blackmail charges against him. Defendant cross-appealed the district court's decision to deny his motion in limine by which defendant contended that any statements he made on the House floor was protected under the Speech and Debate Clause of the Kansas Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a state representative who made statements to another representative on the House floor during a vote on a bill. The State contended that defendant attempted to blackmail the representative into giving a certain vote on the bill. In a preliminary hearing on the charges, defendant's motion in limine to exclude from evidence the statements he made on the floor was denied; however, his motion to dismiss the action was granted. The State's motion to amend the complaint was denied, and both parties appealed. Upon review, the court affirmed the dismissal of the charges because it also determined that defendant's statements on the House floor were protected under the Speech and Debate Clause of the Kansas Constitution, at Kan. Const. art. 2, § 22. The court held that defendant's statements were protected under the state constitution because his utterance on the legislative floor, which related to a pending vote on a legislative bill was a legislative act, regardless of the content of the statement. Thus, the court held, because the statements could not be introduced as evidence, the State was left without any evidence to proceed with the blackmail charges.


OUTCOME: The court held that defendant's statements on the legislative floor should have been excluded from evidence and that they were protected under the Speech and Debate Clause. Thus, the court affirmed the dismissal of the blackmail charges against defendant.

State v. Coffman


No. 74,437


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 811; 925 P.2d 419; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 130



October 25, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court, TOM MALONE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Sedgwick district court (Kansas), which convicted him of two counts of aggravated criminal sodomy under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3506 (1988) for oral sexual acts he perpetrated on a neighbor's six-year-old girl.


OVERVIEW: Defendant perpetrated oral sexual acts on his neighbor's six-year-old daughter. At trial, defendant objected to the instruction on a lesser included offense and he was convicted of two counts of aggravated criminal sodomy. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that, pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3107(3), he waived his objection to the trial court's failure to instruct on indecent liberties with a child by objecting to the instruction. The court held a contention § 21-3701(3) unconstitutionally invaded the province of the jury was not preserved for assertion on appeal. The court rejected a contention the evidence was insufficient to support the convictions because no direct evidence established that defendant was not married to the six-year old victim. The court stated common law provided that the minimum age for females to marry in Kansas was 12 years old, therefore circumstantial evidence conclusively established that defendant was not married to the girl he victimized. The court stated that even the gravest of crimes could be proven by circumstantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of two counts of aggravated criminal sodomy for oral sexual acts he perpetrated on the six-year-old girl.

 

63 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. Harlin


No. 75,234


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 881; 925 P.2d 1149; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 139



October 25, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Ellsworth district court, BARRY A. BENNINGTON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged orders from the Ellsworth District Court (Kansas), which dismissed criminal cases filed against defendants on the grounds that they were violative of the Double Jeopardy Clause of the United States and Kansas Constitutions because defendants had previously been administratively disciplined in prison for the same crimes.


OVERVIEW: Two defendants were charged with battery and one was charged with drug-related crimes. All three defendants had been found guilty of their respective crimes in prison disciplinary proceedings and had been sentenced to segregation, restriction of privileges, and fines. The district court ruled that the Double Jeopardy Clause precluded criminal prosecution for the crimes for which the inmates had already been disciplined. In reversing the district court's orders of dismissal, the court ruled that the subsequent criminal prosecutions were not barred by double jeopardy. The determinative issue was whether the preceding administrative disciplinary penalties constituted "punishment" under recent jurisprudence. The court ruled that the prison discipline imposed did not rise to the level of punishment or multiple prosecution for double jeopardy purposes. Disciplinary rules and procedures were necessary for the control of dangerous and desperate individuals unwillingly confined in such institutions, in order to protect employees, inmates, and visitors. The general rule that prison discipline did not bar subsequent prosecution under criminal laws for the same conduct remained.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of dismissal from the district court and remanded for further proceedings.

Although defendant's argument that the evidence of prior drug sales was inadmissible under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-455 did not affect the appellate court's result, the court held that it had merit. The court deleted the appellate court's language indicating the evidence would be admissible under § 60-455.

State v. Gaines


No. 72,708


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 752; 926 P.2d 641; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 144



October 25, 1996, Opinion filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Gaines v. State, 2003 Kan. App. Unpub. LEXIS 493 (Kan. Ct. App., June 13, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court; PETER V. RUDDICK, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a judgment of the District Court of Johnson County (Kansas) convicting him of rape, aggravated kidnapping, and aggravated criminal sodomy.


OVERVIEW: The victim, a 14-year old girl, was sexually assaulted in her neighborhood. During the attack, defendant sucked the girl's right big toe as if he were sucking on a thumb. In his defense, defendant tried to admit the testimony of an expert witness on the issue of eyewitness identification. The trial court excluded the testimony. On appeal, defendant challenged the exclusion of the expert testimony. The court held that expert testimony regarding eyewitness identification should not be admitted into trial. The court noted that such expert testimony pertained to matters of common knowledge, which were not beyond the ken of lay jurors, making expert testimony unnecessary. Defendant also took issue with the photographic lineup from which he was identified, arguing that it was unduly suggestive. The court held that defendant's failure to object at trial to the second photographic lineup, the procedures used to create the lineup, and his subsequent identification from the lineup precluded review of the issues on appeal. The trial court also did not abuse its discretion in admitting defendant's ex-wife's testimony regarding his participation in toe sucking as such testimony was relevant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction.

 

66 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. Myers


No. 74,078


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 669; 923 P.2d 1024; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 127



August 23, 1996, Opinion filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected September 10, 1996. Certiorari Denied June 27, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4064.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Lyon district court; PAUL W. CLARK, assigned judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Lyon District Court (Kansas), denying his motion to eliminate the requirement that he register as a sex offender pursuant to the Kansas Sex Offender Registration Act (Act), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4901 et seq., after pleading no contest to aggravated sexual battery. He contended that the Act was ex post facto legislation violating U.S. Const. art. I, § 10.


OVERVIEW: After entering a plea of no contest to an aggravated sexual battery charge, defendant was required to register as a sex offender pursuant to the Kansas Sex Offender Registration Act (Act), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 22-4901 et seq. He contended that, as applied to him, the Act violated the ex post facto clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10. His motion to eliminate the requirement was denied and he appealed. The determinative issue was whether the Act constitutionally could be applied to defendant, whose offense was committed before April 14, 1994, the date the Act took effect. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court upheld the constitutionality of the registration requirement in the Act, for it was remedial not punitive. However, when applied to defendant, the disclosure provision allowing public access to sex offender registered information was unconstitutional punishment under the Ex Post Facto Clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the district court's order denying defendant's motion to eliminate the requirement that he register as a sex offender pursuant to the Kansas Sex Offender Registration Act (Act), after he pled no contest to aggravated sexual battery. The Act was constitutional because it was not punitive. However, the disclosure provision was unconstitutional punishment under the Ex Post Facto Clause.

 

67 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rinck


No. 74,537


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 634; 923 P.2d 67; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 124



August 9, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Wyandotte district court, PHILIP L. SIEVE, judge.



DISPOSITION: Sentences vacated and remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the resentencing order by the Wyandotte District Court (Kansas) after it had been determined that defendant's conviction for aggravated battery was a multiplicity with his aggravated robbery conviction.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to the resentencing of defendant by the district court, defendant appealed contending that he was denied due process because his sentences for the same conviction upon remand were more severe than the punishment originally imposed and that the district court erred in its application of the Habitual Criminal Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-4504 (Supp. 1992). Vacating the sentences imposed upon resentencing, the court agreed with defendant's arguments. First, the court held that the sentences imposed violated defendant's due process rights. In order to protect defendant's Due Process Rights under U.S. Const. amend, XIV, the district court was required to the reasons why he imposed a harsher sentence upon resentencing than was originally imposed upon conviction. This removed any thought of vindictiveness against defendant for having successfully attacked his first conviction. The district court failed to articulate his reasons for the more sever sentence. Furthermore, the court found that the district court erred in applying § 21-4504 because it used multiple convictions obtained on the same date for the purpose of sentence enhancement.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence imposed by the district court upon resentencing and remanded defendant's case for resentencing.

 

68 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


Shirley v. Reif


No. 75,044


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 514; 920 P.2d 405; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 113



July 12, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; PAUL BUCHANAN judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed a decision from the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which granted summary judgment to defendants, a bishop and a priest, finding that plaintiffs' claims to recover damages for sexual abuse by the priest were barred by the one-year statute of limitations under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-515(a) and could not be revived by Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-523.


OVERVIEW: When plaintiffs filed their claim, they were both over 21 years of age. On review the court agreed that plaintiffs could not maintain a claim under § 60-515(a), because, under that statute, they were required to bring a claim within one-year after they reached their 18th birthdays. Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-523 extended the period in which to bring childhood sexual abuse claims to within either three years of the person attaining age 18, or within three years from the date plaintiffs discovered the injury. The bishop contended that plaintiffs' claims were dead when § 60-523 was enacted and that the claims could not be revived without violating the constitution. The court distinguished that part of § 60-515(a) that was a statute of repose, which barred any claim brought after eight years after the time of the abuse, and that part of § 60-515(a) that was a statute of limitations, which required suit be brought within one year of reaching age 18. Because the claims were not barred by the statute of repose at the time the revival statute, § 60-523, was enacted, the claims were not barred.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

 

69 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


Ripley v. Tolbert


No. 74,583


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 491; 921 P.2d 1210; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 116



July 12, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; D. KEITH ANDERSON, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant daughter challenged an order from Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which dismissed her claims of sexual abuse under Kan. Stat. Ann § 60-523 (1992) against appellee parents on the basis that her claims were extinguished by virtue of the statute of repose found in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-515(a).


OVERVIEW: The 50-year-old daughter filed an action against her parents under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 60-523 (1992), alleging that less than three years prior to the filing of the lawsuit, she had spontaneously remembered that her father sexually abused her as a minor and that her mother had failed to protect her. On appeal of the lower court's dismissal of her action, the daughter argued that her action did not accrue until she remembered the abuse and that § 60-515(a), which included the eight-year statute of repose upon which the lower court relied, did not apply to her. The court disagreed, finding that the eight-year statute of repose contained in § 60-515(a) applied to persons who were minors at the time of the act. The daughter nonetheless argued that § 60-523, which was passed after the statute of repose expired, revived her claim. The court ruled that while the legislature could pass a law to retroactively revive actions barred by statute of limitations, it could not, without violating due process, retroactively revive a cause of action already barred by a statute of repose. Such action, found the court, would be deprivation of a defendant's property without due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the lower court properly found that the daughter's claims had been extinguished by an eight-year statute of repose.

In re Appeal of Scholastic Book Clubs


No. 75,199


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 528; 920 P.2d 947; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 117; 71 A.L.R.5th 799



July 12, 1996, Opinion filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended August 2, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Board of Tax Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff book club, an out-of-state based business, sold books to elementary school teachers and students throughout the United States. The Kansas Department of Revenue (KDR) assessed a use tax on the gross receipts of the book club's sales in Kansas under the Kansas Compensating Tax Act (KCTA), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-3701 et seq. The Director of Taxation (Director) upheld the tax and the Board of Tax Appeals (BOTA) affirmed the Director's order.


OVERVIEW: The book club claimed that it was not subject to the compensating tax because it was not a retailer doing business in the state of Kansas and did not have a nexus with the state. After reviewing the facts, this court concluded that application of the KCTA did not violate the Commerce Clause. Although the book club did not have bank accounts in Kansas and none of its employees conducted business in the state, the book club had more of a connection with Kansas than catalog sales through the mail or by common carrier. The book club's use of the Kansas teachers to sell their product to Kansas students provided a substantial nexus with the state of Kansas that permitted the conclusion that it was a retailer doing business in the state.


OUTCOME: The BOTA's order affirming the assessment of a use tax was affirmed.

State v. Webber


No. 73,991


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 263; 918 P.2d 609; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 100



June 7, 1996, Opinion filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied, January 21, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 525.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Stevens district court; TOM R. SMITH, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Stevens District Court (Kansas), which convicted her of one count of aiding and abetting first-degree murder under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3401 and one count of conspiracy to commit first-degree murder under § 21-3302; 21-3401. Defendant was sentenced to concurrent sentences of 40 years' and 73 months' imprisonment, respectively.


OVERVIEW: Evidence indicated that defendant desired to have her husband killed to resolve a child custody dispute and avoid legal fees. Defendant allegedly conspired with her boyfriend, who allegedly shot and killed defendant's husband. She was convicted of aiding and abetting murder and conspiracy to commit murder. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court correctly admitted evidence that defendant fled the state, travelled to Louisiana under an assumed name, and disguised herself. The court held that evidence that defendant had a large sum of money at the time of her arrest was also admissible. The court stated that evidence that she took a polygraph prior to leaving the state was inadmissible because its admission would have implied that the results of the test were favorable to defendant. The court further held that a bill of particulars was sufficient, the prosecutor's remark regarding defendant's post-arrest silence did not constitute reversible error, and the evidence was sufficient. The court held that testimony that defendant had previously inquired into whether a person would kill her husband was admissible and other trial rulings were proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of one count of aiding and abetting first-degree murder and one count of conspiracy to commit first-degree murder, and sentenced her to concurrent terms of 40 years' and 73 months' imprisonment, respectively.

Robinson v. Kansas State High Sch. Activities Ass'n


No. 74,817


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 136; 917 P.2d 836; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 84



May 31, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; C. ROBERT BELL, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant high school activities association (association) appealed the judgment of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which held that the eligibility rules of the association violated the Kan. Const. art. II, § 1 as an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power. Plaintiff fathers of children involved in interscholastic athletics (fathers) obtained a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: The fathers of children involved in interscholastic athletics sued the association. The association's rules prohibited the high school basketball players from attending competitive summer basketball camps, from concurrently playing on non-school teams, or from receiving private instruction from non-school personnel. The association was organized under Kan. Stat. Ann. § 72-130 (1995) and handled interscholastic athletics for approximately 90 percent of Kansas high schools. The court first agreed with the district court that the fathers met the minimal requirements for standing under the Declaratory Judgment Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 61-1701 et seq. However, the court reversed the district court's holding that the association's rules were void ab initio as an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power. The legislature did not impose any statutory requirements on the association and did not delegate or sub-delegate rule-making authority to the association. Rather, it was a nonprofit corporation consisting of voluntary members. The association adopted its own rules, and high schools were not required to join the association and could withdraw their membership at any time.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, holding that the fathers had standing, but that the association's rules were not void and unenforceable as an unconstitutional delegation or sub-delegation of legislative power.

 

73 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. Copridge


No. 72,761


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 19; 918 P.2d 1247; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 96



May 31, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court, RICHARD T. BALLINGER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which entered convictions for felony murder, first-degree murder, aggravated robbery, and aggravated kidnapping. Defendant received a hard 40 sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was in jail on an unrelated offense when he became a suspect in the murder investigation. The police searched his personal effects for purposes of the murder investigation and then obtained blood and hair samples when they noticed blood on defendant's shoe. An attorney represented defendant on the unrelated charge. Even though the police told defendant they could not talk about the murder with him because of the representation, defendant insisted on talking about the murder. On appeal, defendant claimed that the trial court erred in failing to suppress evidence. The court affirmed the judgment. In doing so, the court held that the trial court properly refused to suppress the evidence collected from a search of defendant's personal effects because such effects were lawfully seized and subject to search without a warrant or probable cause. The prosecutor's statement that defendant was in jail on an unrelated offense did not violate the motion in limine because the fact that defendant was in jail explained his location when his clothing was searched by the police and when the police talked to him about the crime.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Allen


No. 74,639


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


260 Kan. 107; 917 P.2d 848; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 82



May 31, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Johnson district court; JOHN ANDERSON III, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of the judgment from the Johnson District Court (Kansas), which dismissed the criminal complaint against defendant after a preliminary hearing. The criminal complaint charged that defendant's telephonic connections prompted a corporate computer owner to change its security systems, thus constituting felony computer crime in violation of Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3755(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant had used his computer, equipped with a modem, to call various modems of the corporate computer owner, using random dialing. The State presented no evidence that defendant had ever entered any computer system of the corporate computer owner. The trial court dismissed the complaint after finding no probable cause existed to believe defendant had committed any crime. On appeal, the court determined that felony computer crime pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 21-3755(b)(1) required that the State prove three distinct elements: (1) intentional and unauthorized access to a computer system, computer network, or any other defined property; (2) damage to a computer, computer system, computer network, or any other defined property; and (3) a loss in value as the result of such crime of at least $ 500 but less than $ 25,000. The court held that the trial court did not err in determining that the State had failed to present evidence showing probable cause as to any of the three elements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court dismissing the criminal complaint for lack of probable cause.

State v. Weaver


No. 74,233


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


259 Kan. 844; 915 P.2d 746; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 62



April 19, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Reno district court; STEVEN R. BECKER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed an order of the Reno District Court (Kansas), which suppressed evidence seized by an officer following a chase and apprehension of defendant, and which dismissed the State's case for driving while under the influence of alcohol or drugs, fleeing or attempting to elude a law enforcement officer, and consumption of an alcoholic beverage by a minor, without prejudice.


OVERVIEW: An officer was standing outside his patrol car when he saw defendant's vehicle driving down the road, turning its headlights off and on. When the vehicle passed him, he followed it. The vehicle did not weave or do anything illegal. The officer decided to pull it over to determine why it was flashing its lights. When the officer activated his lights, the vehicle fled. A chase ensued, during which the vehicle drove through a private yard, went through a garden, and drove into a wheat field where it finally stopped. The officer chased the driver and arrested him. The trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress, holding that at the time the officer turned on his lights to stop defendant, he had no probable cause to do so. Anything that happened after that time was not considered in determining whether he had probable cause to stop defendant. The court held that for purposes of determining whether probable cause existed to stop defendant, the "stop" did not occur until defendant submitted to the officer's authority, which happened in the field after the chase. The "stop" was reasonable and the evidence admissible.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order suppressing the evidence and dismissing the charges against defendant, and remanded for a reconsideration of the officer's reasonable suspicion based on the circumstances.

In re Raborn


Nos. 73,779, 73,781, 73,782


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


259 Kan. 813; 916 P.2d 15; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 56



April 19, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; PAUL W. CLARK, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a judgment from the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which required it to pay the fees of appointed counsel and expert witnesses in actions brought by the State against three respondents under the Sexually Violent Predator Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-29a01 et seq. (Supp. 1995). The county also disputed the fee rates approved by the district court.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the county argued that (1) it should not have been held responsible for the payment of fees for appointed counsel and expert witnesses under the Sexually Violent Predator Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-29a01 et seq. (Supp. 1995); and (2) it did not acquiesce in the judgment of the district court by paying certain fee claims and paying at the rates set by the district court. Upon review, the court found the county's arguments without merit. Kan. Stat. Ann. § 20-348 made each county responsible for all expenses incurred for the operation of its district court except expenses that the law required the State to pay. Expenses incurred for the operation of the district court included fees for court-appointed counsel to represent indigent defendants in matters where the State was not required by law to pay. The fees incurred in representing three respondents in sexual predator proceedings were expenses incurred for the operation of the district court and, as such, were to be paid by the county.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the district court.

 

77 of 1377 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jensen


No. 73,406, No. 73,467, No. 73,578


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


259 Kan. 781; 915 P.2d 109; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 47



April 19, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Ellis district court; EDWARD BOUKER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of a judgment from the Ellis District Court (Kansas), which dismissed the possession of marijuana charges in two separate criminal cases against defendants on the basis of double jeopardy. One defendant sought review of his conviction from the district court that convicted him of marijuana possession and that denied his motion to suppress. The cases were consolidated on appeal.


OVERVIEW: An informant told the police officers that defendant was growing marijuana in his basement. The informant went before a judge so that the officers could obtain a search warrant. The search revealed the presence of marijuana, but not a growing operation in the basement. The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession because his motion to suppress was properly denied. Even though defendant was allowed to attack an affidavit that supported a search warrant if the affidavit contained deliberate falsehoods, the rule did not apply because the falsehoods had to be made by a government agent. The court reversed the district court's judgment that dismissed the State's cases because the payment of a tax under the Kansas Drug Tax Act, Kan. Stat. Ann. § 79-5201 et seq. barred subsequent criminal prosecution on charges that arose from possessing drugs. The Double Jeopardy Clause of the United States Constitution and Kan. Const. Bill of Rights § 10 did not bar the subsequent criminal prosecution because a civil sanction only invoked double jeopardy protections if it was grossly disproportional to legitimate state goals that were separate from those served by criminal prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment that convicted defendant of drug possession. The court reversed and remanded the district court's judgments that dismissed the State's cases on the basis of double jeopardy and remanded the actions to the district court with directions to reinstate the charges against defendants.

Moorhouse v. City of Wichita


No. 73,436


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


259 Kan. 570; 913 P.2d 172; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 42



March 15, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; PAUL BUCHANAN, judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee brought an action against defendants, city and county, which alleged breach of contract; she sought mandamus and requested specific performance. The Sedgwick District Court (Kansas) granted defendants' motion for summary judgment. The employee filed an appeal. The case was transferred to the court pursuant to Kan. Stat. Ann. § 20-3018(c).


OVERVIEW: The employee had been employed by the consolidated City of Wichita and Sedgwick County Emergency Communications Department (ECD) for approximately 18 years. Operation of the consolidated ECD was transferred to the county. The employee became a county employee. Based on her reduced retirement benefits, the employee filed a complaint. She later returned to city employment and was reinstated in the city's plan. The employee filed an amended complaint. The city and the county filed motions for summary judgment, which the trial court granted. The court affirmed. The employee lacked standing. She had returned to the city and rejoined the plan. Even if the joint ordinance/resolution had been unconstitutional, the employee no longer had a personal stake in the outcome of the controversy and could obtain no relief from such a finding. In addition, the alternative relief sought, specific performance, was unnecessary. She had returned to the city and was participating in the retirement plan. The employee's lower expected benefits were the result of her lower salary, not because of any modification by the city. The county was not liable; it was a separate legal entity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

OVERVIEW: The boards filed for a temporary injunction to stop the midwife from continuing her alleged practice of medicine and nursing. As to the midwife, the court found that her activities that she may have engaged in beyond midwifery were excepted from the healing arts act by virtue of the supervision provided by a licensed physician.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that denied the boards' request for a temporary injunction and reversed the trial court's order that the acts at issue violated the constitution.

City of Wichita v. Public Emple. Rels. Bd.


No. 74,216


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


259 Kan. 628; 913 P.2d 137; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 39



March 15, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court, C. ROBERT BELL, judge.
City of Wichita v. Public Emple. Rels. Bd. of the Kan. Dep't of Human Res., 1994 Kan. Unpub. LEXIS 25 (Kan., Oct. 28, 1994)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged an order of the Sedwick District Court (Kansas) holding that the city and airport authority were not separate entities as held by appellee Public Employee Relations Board (PERB) but were a single employer subject to the Public Employer-Employee Relations Act (PEERA), Kan. Stat. Ann. § 75-4321 et seq. The trial court affirmed PERB's decision that it had jurisdiction to determine the petitioned for unit determination.


OVERVIEW: Appellee union filed a petition with the PERB seeking to become the exclusive bargaining representative for certain safety officers working at the city airport. The petition alleged that the city was the employer of the officers and that they were subject to PEERA. The city argued that the officers were the employees of the airport authority, which was not subject to PEERA. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. The city and airport authority were a single employer subject to PERRA. The PERB had jurisdiction to determine the petitioned for unit determination. The court reasoned that the city actively controlled the labor relations and working conditions of employees at the airport authority by establishing position descriptions, setting personnel policy and benefits, and including the airport authority in the self-insured fund administered by the city. The city had sufficient control to afford meaningful negotiations under PEERA.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

In re Care & Treatment of Hendricks


No. 73,039


SUPREME COURT OF KANSAS


259 Kan. 246; 912 P.2d 129; 1996 Kan. LEXIS 21



March 1, 1996, Opinion filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Sedgwick district court; GREGORY L. WALLER, judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Sedgwick District Court (Kansas), which found that he was a sexually violent predator and ordered him committed to a state hospital.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found to be a sexually violent predator as defined in Kan. Stat. Ann. § 59-29a01 and he was committed to a state hospital. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the Sexually Violent Predator Act (the Act) and also raised various other ground for reversing the finding and order of the trial court. He argued that the Act violated the prohibition against double jeopardy and ex post facto laws, failed to prove equal protection and procedural and substantive due process, and was void as overly broad and vague. The court stated that the record indicated that defendant had at least three felony convictions prior to being charged in the present case. Under the Habitual Criminal Act, defendant's sentence could have been tripled. Additionally, the sentences could have been ordered to run consecutively rather than concurrently. However, the court also stated that the state's own evidence was that defendant was being committed even though he did not suffer from mental illness. Defendant was not mentally ill, and the criminal offenses he committed were not the result of mental illness. Therefore, the Act violated the Due Process Clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, which ordered defendant committed to a mental facility. The court held that defendant's commitment violated the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.
